# Spss Wrangling Visualizing And Modeling Data

> SPSS is a common menu-driven statistical application used in the social sciences, education, and business. In this course, data analytics expert Barton Poulson shows you how to analyze data in ways that are difficult or impossible in spreadsheets, without requiring the full learning curve of a programming language like Python or R. Learn how to navigate SPSS, use sample datasets, set options and p

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data) | Intermediate | 105K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

### 2. 1. What is SPSS?

#### SPSS in context
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] There's a small store down the hill from me in Salt Lake City, Utah, that does brisk international business in selling t-shirts and sweatshirts to fans of heavy metal music.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=11)** For over 35 years, they've kept track of their sales on yellow legal pads, and while writing things down is a great first step to tracking your data, you can do a lot more with your data once you get it into a computer.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=24)** Now, for most people and most situations, that will mean a spreadsheet like Microsoft Excel or Google Sheets, and depending on your purposes, that may be all that you ever need.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=36)** But there are some things that spreadsheets don't do very easily, like the analysis of variants, multiple regression or hierarchical clustering, and that's where statistical applications like SBSS come in.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=49)** SBSS is a popular statistical application that was developed over 50 years ago.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=54)** The name SBSS originally stood for a statistical package for the social sciences, and it was the name of both the program and the company that originally developed it before they sold it to IBM.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=65)** There was also a brief period in 2009 when SPSS was referred to as PASW for predictive analytics software, and I have seen people say that SPSS now stands for a statistical product and service solutions, but IBM just calls it SPSS.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=81)** More accurately. IBM calls it IBM SPS statistics because IBM has other data applications like IBM SPS modeler for data mining and text, IBM SPSS Amos for structural equation modeling, not to mention non SBSS apps like IBM Cognos Analytics for Business Intelligence, IBM, Watson Studio for AI and so on.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=106)** SBSS has long been a popular choice for data work in the social sciences and education and marketing, as well as fields like healthcare and finance and so on.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=117)** Other apps are also useful.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=119)** They can include SaaS and Minitab.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=122)** They can include PSPP, which is a play on the name SPSS.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=127)** It's a free open source version, I've used it.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=130)** I find it a little difficult to use.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=132)** On the other hand, I'm a huge fan of the program's JASP and Jamovi, particularly Jamovi, which are both free open source applications that resemble SPSS, but run on different languages.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=144)** Jamovi runs on R and I strongly recommend it as a way of bridging between the two.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=150)** Then there are languages like R and Python, which are used very often for data work.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=157)** And of course, SaaS is both an app and a programming language.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=161)** And the Julia Programming language, which is developed for data work as well.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=164)** They give you an enormous amount of power and flexibility, both at the cost of having to learn how to write a programming language, but that may not be on the list of people who are focusing on their research and other tasks at hand.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/spss-in-context?u=76281980&t=179)** SBSS hits a nice balance between capability of a statistically oriented program and the usability of a menu driven interface, a sweet spot that has made it very popular for many years.

#### Versions, releases, licenses, and interfaces
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] SPSS is designed to make your analytical life easy so you can just kick back and get valuable insight from your data, but sometimes keeping track of all the variations of SPSS feels a little more like this.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=15)** That's because SPSS has been around a long time and varies in at least four different ways, not counting the operating system you're on.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=23)** There are different versions, different releases, different licenses, and different interfaces, and I want to talk a little bit about each of these.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=32)** First, versions.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=34)** Now, SPSS Version 1 came out in 1968, and then it was PASW from 2009 to 2010, and right now we have SPSS Statistics Version 30, which was released in 2024.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=49)** But there is also an SPSS Statistics subscription, which is sort of a version, free version.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=57)** In addition to that, there are different releases, different ways of leveling up what you're able to do.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=63)** There is the Base, which is adequate for many situations, but the Standard, which is different from Base, Standard adds logistic regression and invasion statistics.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=75)** Professional adds automated data preparation, principal component analysis, decision trees.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=81)** And Premium has bootstrapping, some basic versions of neural networks, and Amos for structural equation modeling, and that's the version, Premium, that I'm using for this course, although I won't be using many of the advanced functions in this particular course.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=96)** There are also add-ins for subscriptions, and there's a lot of other versions that you can use.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=103)** In addition, there are licenses.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=105)** You can get a subscription that's monthly or annual, and they can cost real money starting at $99 per month for license up to thousands of dollars per person per year.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=118)** Now, education licenses are much cheaper, they're more affordable, but they only apply to some customers, and so you have to think about whether you want a subscription, whether you want a perpetual license, a term license, one user, or multiple users.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=132)** And even if you're into education, you have to choose between the Campus Edition, the GradPack, the faculty pack, and so there's a lot of options to go through things.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=142)** And then finally, there are interfaces.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=145)** Now, some time ago, a few years ago, SPS developed what they call the quote, unquote, "new user interface," and it was available for certain functions.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=156)** There's also the quote, unquote, "classic user interface."
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=159)** We're going to be using the classic user interface for a few reasons.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=163)** Number one is, I can't tell that the new one is readily available.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=169)** Even when it was being actively promoted, it was for subscription only and not for people who purchased the application.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=176)** The vast majority of people who use SPSS use the quote, unquote "classic interface."
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=181)** Also, the new interface didn't include all the functions.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=183)** There were things you simply couldn't do with it, and you had to go back to the classic interface, and there was inconsistent feedback on it, and so I don't know if it's still on IBM's plans, but we won't be using it.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/versions-releases-licenses-and-interfaces?u=76281980&t=196)** But hopefully, with this information, you can sort through the tangle of options and find the one version of SPSS that works best for you.

### 3. 2. Getting Started

#### Navigating SPSS
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] When you first open SPSS, what you're going to see is this collection of windows.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=5)** Here on the top, is the Welcome Dialogue.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=9)** Now this is a nice place to start, 'cause it gives you some options to get you up and running very quickly.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=14)** For instance, right here, it shows you the recent files you've used.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=19)** They can be data files, they can be output files, many different kinds.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=24)** If you've been working and you lost some of your work, you can click on Restore Points, and they can do an auto recovery of what you've done previously.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=32)** Probably one of the nicest things about the Welcome Dialogue, is that this is the easiest way to access the sample data files that are built into SPSS.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=41)** You just click on Sample Files, and you've got the full list of what's available here.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=47)** Now, it also gives you a link to Help & Support, to the tutorials provided by SPSS and to the larger community, as well as quick shots of new features in SPSS.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=60)** Now, if you don't want this open all the time, you can come over here and click Don't show this dialogue in the future.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=69)** And then you can close it either by clicking on this standard red close dot, or come into the bottom-right, and hitting Close.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=77)** But before I do that, I'm going to use this to open up a recent file.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=82)** Demo is one of the built-in sample files, and I'm going to use that to show you a little bit about how some of the other windows in SPSS work.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=91)** So, I double click on that.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=93)** And what we have here is a few different dancing screens.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=98)** Now I want you to be aware that there's more than one window open right now.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=103)** If I come down, if I make this window a little smaller, you'll see that this is our data window.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=109)** It's got the rows and columns of data like a spreadsheet, but we also have an Output window where it puts the results of our work.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=118)** And if I make that smaller, there's a third window it's called a Syntax window.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/navigating-spss?u=76281980&t=123)** Now, that opens because I changed the settings to Always open a syntax window.
>

> **CLI Commands:** make (7), find (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (8)
> **Cross-References:** go back to (6)
> **Analogies:** for instance (3)

#### The SPSS workbook interface
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/the-spss-workbook-interface?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/the-spss-workbook-interface?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In general, SPSS is a very stable piece of software where each version strongly resembles the one that came before it, with some small changes around the edges and sometimes some important functionality.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/the-spss-workbook-interface?u=76281980&t=12)** One of the interesting recent changes is the addition of what's called a workbook view, and I want to give you a quick overview of the workbook view, although I'll also say we won't be using it for this course because of some of the limitations it has.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/the-spss-workbook-interface?u=76281980&t=28)** And what a workbook is, is a single document that combines formatted text, syntax and output, as opposed to having a separate output window and a separate syntax window.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/the-spss-workbook-interface?u=76281980&t=38)** Now the data still lives in its own window, the rows and columns that we've had before, but the idea here is that you can have a single unified document for conducting your work and seeing the results of your work.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/the-spss-workbook-interface?u=76281980&t=51)** Other languages have interactive notebooks that allow you to combine text in your results, and so this is SPSS's attempt at the same general idea.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/the-spss-workbook-interface?u=76281980&t=60)** Now, to enable this, you first need to go to settings or preferences, and right here under application mode, you probably have classic, that's syntax and output, already checked.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/the-spss-workbook-interface?u=76281980&t=74)** If you check workbook and then come down and press okay, it says it's going to close everything else you've got, and then it will open up a new workbook.

> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/the-spss-workbook-interface?u=76281980&t=85)** Now we've got the workbook open here.

>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/the-spss-workbook-interface?u=76281980&t=151)** The important thing, and this is one of the main selling points, is that if you modify the syntax and you press run syntax, it will replace the old output.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/the-spss-workbook-interface?u=76281980&t=160)** So it's easier to modify and overwrite what you did previously as long as you're comfortable writing syntax statements.
>

#### Modifying Graphboard visualizations
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/modifying-graphboard-visualizations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/modifying-graphboard-visualizations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The Graphboard Template Chooser and SPSS is a great way to automatically get recommendations for the kind of visualizations that match your data, but you also have the opportunity to customize them.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/modifying-graphboard-visualizations?u=76281980&t=12)** And in fact, there's some really wonderful options that may not be immediately apparent when you're using this function.

> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/using-legacy-dialogs-boxplots-for-multiple-variables?u=76281980&t=204)** So take a look at the options you have.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/using-legacy-dialogs-boxplots-for-multiple-variables?u=76281980&t=206)** Some are very automated and very integrated, other ones are very specific.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/using-legacy-dialogs-boxplots-for-multiple-variables?u=76281980&t=211)** And you want to choose the ones that are going to allow you to do what you want and do it in a way that is effective and efficient for you to get insight from your data.

>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-regression-variable-plots?u=76281980&t=9)** And it's right here if you have it in your edition.

> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-regression-variable-plots?u=76281980&t=14)** And then the Regressions Variables Plot is a way at looking at the associations of variables that you might use in a regression model.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-regression-variable-plots?u=76281980&t=21)** And what's neat about it is you pick the vertical axis variables, that's the outcome variables, the Y axis that's usually the thing you're trying to predict.
>

>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-regression-variable-plots?u=76281980&t=40)** So let's do this, let's take for instance, something like household income and we'll make that an outcome we're trying to predict and maybe job satisfaction.

> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/splitting-files?u=76281980&t=331)** Now the other option we have is instead of comparing them this way, let's go back to the Split File option, 'cause what we're doing right now is we're comparing groups.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/splitting-files?u=76281980&t=342)** You can also organize the output by groups, and we can do that by selecting organize output by groups and getting this psychological region, putting that here in our groups, and we can hit OK.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/splitting-files?u=76281980&t=357)** And I already have it in the syntax, so I'm going to press Cancel and go to the syntax, and we're going to sort it a little differently.

>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/splitting-files?u=76281980&t=379)** I'll hit run on that one, and then let's do our two commands over again.

> **[6:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/splitting-files?u=76281980&t=410)** Now, if you're going to be doing, say for instance, correlation matrices, it might show up very differently here, but this is a nice way to say, "Show me each group by itself, but do them one right after the other."
>
> **[7:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/splitting-files?u=76281980&t=424)** And so SPLIT FILE and the two different options of comparing and organizing allow you to focus on these group differences and how they affect things like the distribution of variables, the descriptive statistics, or other statistics that you might be looking at.
>
> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/splitting-files?u=76281980&t=440)** It's a good way to get more focused insight into your data.

#### Recoding by ranking cases
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-by-ranking-cases?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-by-ranking-cases?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the most intrusive, disturbing things that can happen in data analysis is outliers.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-by-ranking-cases?u=76281980&t=7)** These are extreme values on a high end or a low end because they throw off things like the mean, and they throw off correlations, they throw off regression coefficients.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-by-ranking-cases?u=76281980&t=17)** Outliers occur in real life, but if you're not aware that they're there in your data, you can have problems.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-by-ranking-cases?u=76281980&t=24)** And so you need to both be aware that they're there and have some way of dealing with them, and ranking, which is very easy to do in SPSS, is one way to deal with that.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-by-ranking-cases?u=76281980&t=35)** I want to start by looking at some of the Google Trends search terms that we have.

>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-by-ranking-cases?u=76281980&t=172)** The other question is what to do about ties.

>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-by-ranking-cases?u=76281980&t=181)** I like to give it the lower value.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-by-ranking-cases?u=76281980&t=182)** So for instance, if you think about the Olympics, if you have two people tied for second place, they both get second, not third, and so I'm going to give it to the lower ones.

> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-by-ranking-cases?u=76281980&t=191)** We'll hit Continue.

#### Creating dummy variables
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Data is really a lot more flexible than people assume, and ideas like there are quantitative variables and qualitative variables or categories and scales are actually a little more flexible, and one place where this shows up is the use of indicator variables or often called dummy variables.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980&t=19)** I don't like that term.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980&t=20)** I don't know what it means.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980&t=21)** I think it refers to a stand-in like a showroom dummy, but I call them dummy variables because that's what SPSS calls them, but I like to think of them as indicator variables.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980&t=32)** These are 0/1 variables that indicate whether a score is in a particular category, and they can be used to take something that might be text-based and turn it into this numerical variable that can then be used in T-tests and correlations and regressions, and actually gives you a lot more flexibility than you might originally think that you had.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980&t=55)** For example, what I'm going to show you here in the sample dataset is it will take this one variable here, psy_reg for psychological region, which classify states as friendly or creative or uninhibited, and we're going to turn this into three different dummy or indicator variables, each one to say yes/no, it's friendly, yes/no, it's creative, yes/no, it's uninhibited.
>

>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980&t=233)** But another way to create commands in SPSS, and the one that I prefer, I've been using it for years and I find it easy to work with, is to use a combination of COMPUTE and IF commands.
>

>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980&t=277)** So give everybody a zero on friendly, then if they have a one on psy_reg then change that zero to a one and then Execute.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980&t=287)** That says do it, and then I do a similar thing.

>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980&t=310)** So this is a way of going through all the potential values on a variable and then changing them to give a yes, no, and new variables.

> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980&t=365)** It requires the Python functionality and it gives it these names that are slightly cryptic, whereas the approach I give with the COMPUTE and IF gives you a lot more flexibility and control over what things are called and how things are coded.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-dummy-variables?u=76281980&t=379)** But either one is going to take the values on a single categorical variable and turn them into the zeros and ones of variables that are either called dummy variables or indicator variables, and is a good way to give you more flexibility for use in approaches, again, like T-tests, like correlation and regression, and another way of extracting potentially valuable insights from your data.

>
> **[7:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-with-optimal-binning?u=76281980&t=432)** The idea here is that the optimal binning function in SPSS is able to go through the variables and see whether there is a value that evenly splits groups that you're looking for, and to tell you on a data-driven approach what that best value would be for splitting.
>
> **[7:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-with-optimal-binning?u=76281980&t=449)** And so this is one potential tool to use for getting insight into your data and helping you find hopefully something actionable in your analysis.

> **[6:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/preparing-data-for-modeling?u=76281980&t=396)** Now normally 30 predictors in one outcome with 48 cases is going to be a problem.
>
> **[6:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/preparing-data-for-modeling?u=76281980&t=402)** I'm assuming that if you did this, you're going to have thousands of cases.
>
> **[6:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/preparing-data-for-modeling?u=76281980&t=405)** That's going to be more important.
>
> **[6:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/preparing-data-for-modeling?u=76281980&t=408)** But it tells us that it transformed all of them.
>
> **[6:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/preparing-data-for-modeling?u=76281980&t=410)** And we can actually look down here and see how it worked with the fields and what it did in terms of missing values and so on and so forth.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/preparing-data-for-modeling?u=76281980&t=418)** There's a lot of information here, but I can skip over most of it.
>
> **[7:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/preparing-data-for-modeling?u=76281980&t=423)** I'm just going to come down here and hit run.
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/preparing-data-for-modeling?u=76281980&t=427)** Now it takes us to our output window, which is blank, but if we go to our data window, you'll be able to see that if you scroll to the end, we now have a whole collection of new variables that are either reordered categorical variables or are Z-score transformed quantitative variables.
>
> **[7:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/preparing-data-for-modeling?u=76281980&t=451)** Now, this isn't the same thing as actually doing the analysis.
>
> **[7:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/preparing-data-for-modeling?u=76281980&t=454)** That doesn't do the K-nearest neighbors, it doesn't do the regression, but what it does is SPSS's attempt at creating a data set that would work better for those particular approaches.
>
> **[7:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/preparing-data-for-modeling?u=76281980&t=466)** And so depending on what you're trying to do, this could be a very important way of preparing the data for your modeling as long as it facilitates the interpretation of the results to give you the actionable insights you're looking for.
>
> **[8:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/preparing-data-for-modeling?u=76281980&t=482)** And so take the automatic data preparation of SPSS as one potentially useful tool in working with your data and getting insight out of it.

#### Computing scores
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-scores?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-scores?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A final bit of data wrangling I want to show it to you, is how to compute scores either by averaging or by counting variables.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-scores?u=76281980&t=8)** Now, there's a few reasons for doing this.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-scores?u=76281980&t=10)** Number one is sometimes you're working with things that are supposed to be average, you're using a published scale and you need to combine things, but often the real advantage here is that by aggregating your data, not only do you have fewer variables you have to deal with in your analyses, but you reduce the effect of the idiosyncratic noise for each variable.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-scores?u=76281980&t=30)** If you have several variables that are all supposed to measure approximately the same thing, they each bring in their own little bit of stuff with them.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-scores?u=76281980&t=37)** But when you average, those variations tend to average out, and so you tend to get a better impression of the true signal that you're trying to measure in the first place.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-scores?u=76281980&t=48)** So creating scores either by averaging or by counting, can be two good ways of reducing your data and getting a clearer signal of the thing that you're interested in.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-scores?u=76281980&t=62)** In this example, I'm going to be using a toy data set that I created.

#### Exploratory data analysis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/exploratory-data-analysis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/exploratory-data-analysis?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Descriptives are great, but sometimes you need to look at things a little more closely.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/exploratory-data-analysis?u=76281980&t=5)** And that's where we get into what's called exploratory data analysis or EDA.
>

> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=261)** So depending on the nature of your variables, you may want to use one of the rank order ones.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=265)** But whichever one you choose, it's an easy way to get it in SPSS.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=270)** And again, as before, you can do all of this with syntax as well.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=275)** I've saved these commands as syntax.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=277)** I've also pointed out here, by the way, that with the number of variables that we're including in things, we get a lot of false positives.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=285)** And so you need to be careful.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=287)** That's especially when I include all the Google trends, you have 190 unique correlations.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=292)** I restricted it to the big five to make it easier for the other ones.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=296)** But this is a good way, again, for sharing and replicating your research.

#### Factor analysis and principal component analysis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=0)** - In the data world, there's a paradox in that more data is generally better.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=5)** You want more observations for more reliability and more statistical power.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=10)** You want more variables.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=12)** You can get a richer, more nuanced look at what you're dealing with.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=17)** But the trade off is that more variables makes things, aside from just more complicated, it also brings in a lot of potential sources of error.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=27)** Specifically any one variable is going to measure what you want along with some idiosyncratic variance error that's specific to that variable.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=38)** And if you asked a question in a slightly different way, you'd get a little bit different kind of error.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=44)** Now, that's why a lot of surveys ask what seems like the same question lots of different times, but in different ways, because they're hoping that they can find a way through the individual error variance of each variable.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=60)** Now, a good way to deal with this is actually to take your variables and collapse them.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=66)** So you're actually going from say, 10 variables that measure approximately the same thing, down to one or two combined meta variables.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=74)** And the advantage of that, aside from having fewer things to deal with, is that the individual idiosyncratic variance of each variable tends to cancel out and you get a more solid, reliable picture of the thing that you are hoping to measure.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=91)** Now in terms of how to combine things, you can take a purely theory-driven, I want these ones to go together.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=96)** I mean, you can do that, but you can also use a data-driven approach, using the related techniques of factor analysis and principle component analysis.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=106)** These are actually theoretically distinct approaches and they look at the relationship between the individual observed variables and the combined meta variables differently.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=117)** They approach it from one direction or the other, but in theory they have a lot in common and they can be used to serve the same general purposes.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=125)** I want to show you how to do this in SPSS, and we'll do this by looking at our 15 Google Trends search variables.
>

> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=236)** Think about if you're looking at stocks, you might look at one tech stock and another tech stock and a third tech stock.

>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=249)** They'll have like a NASDAQ index, which is mostly tech stocks, but they tend to move together, or they'll build indices for different kinds of stocks that move together, that the one is associated with the other.

>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=282)** And what we're finding here is that there are components here that seem to capture a lot of the variance of the individual variables all at once.

>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=304)** Each variable started with one unit, so you want to have a component that captures more than that.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=310)** And collectively these three explain about 69% of the variance, so that looks pretty good.
>

>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=340)** And you can see that they vary in how strong they are and some are positive and some are negative, but it's a little hard to know what to do with these.

>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=420)** One is to use an iGen value.
>

>

>

>
> **[7:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=475)** And sometimes if you turn things around a little bit, it's easier to make sense of them.

>
> **[8:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=498)** One of the big decisions we have to make right here is whether we're going to use an approach that requires orthogonal or oblique rotations.
>
> **[8:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=507)** Now orthogonal means that each of your resulting dimensions has to be perpendicular to each other, that there is no association between them.
>
> **[8:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=513)** There are times when that makes things a lot simpler, but I also think it makes interpretation a little more difficult.
>
> **[8:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=520)** An oblique rotation, which allows things to be associated, can also be really helpful.
>
> **[8:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=525)** And so I generally use this option right here, called Direct Oblimin, and we're going to apply a Kaiser normalization, which is a way of controlling for differences in the variances, or really the shared communalities between variables.
>
> **[9:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=540)** So that's on there by default, we're just going to leave it the way it is, and then we can also look at a rotated solution.

>
> **[11:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=675)** You can see it shifts around there.
>

> **[11:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=681)** Now, using the criterion of an eigenvalue of at least one, SPSS decided that there should be three components.
>
> **[11:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=689)** And here it's telling us what goes into each of those components.
>
> **[11:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=692)** What it's doing is it's taking the variables, and you can see that it's sorting them in descending value on component one, and then component two, and then component three.
>
> **[11:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=704)** And it's not showing us anything that's less than 0.30.
>
> **[11:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=708)** So it's a little easier to see what's going on.
>
> **[11:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=711)** Now what's happening here is we have something called a pattern matrix.
>
> **[11:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=715)** Below it we have something else called a structure matrix, which is very similar.
>
> **[12:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=722)** Without getting too much into the intricacies of how all this works, you can think of a structure matrix as being like regular correlation coefficients with each of the components, and that's really helpful, but because the variables are associated with each other, you have a little bit of muddiness in looking at the associations.
>
> **[12:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=741)** And this is why using the pattern matrix can be a little clearer, because these aren't straight ahead regular correlations, these are like partial correlations.
>
> **[12:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=751)** And what it's showing us is how much does each variable contribute independently, separate from any overlapping variance it has with anything else.
>
> **[12:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=759)** And so the pattern matrix is generally a clearer way of seeing the individual contribution of each variable to a component.
>
> **[12:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/factor-analysis-and-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=769)** So that's the one that I'm going to be using here.

> **[8:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/reliability-analysis?u=76281980&t=526)** And reliability in this particular context means, how well do these variables go together?

> **[8:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/reliability-analysis?u=76281980&t=532)** Think of it as an average correlation of each item with all of the others.

### 8. 7. Clustering and Classification

#### Hierarchical clustering
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Sometimes when you're working with data, you want to put like with like.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=4)** You want to be a matchmaker and see what cases or observations go together.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=9)** Sometimes that makes it possible for you to do, for instance, market segmentation, and you are going to do online appeals to different groups, but you can only do so many.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=18)** Other times, you're designing some kind of intervention to help, for instance, different schools, and you want to know which ones are similar enough that you can use a similar approach to them.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=30)** One way of doing this is with hierarchical classification.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=36)** This is a method that allows you to see a branching model of the similarities between the cases based on the variables that you feed to it, and then from that, make some conclusions about what level of division and similarities is most similar and how you can implement that in your work.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=56)** Fortunately, this is really easy to do in SPSS.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=59)** I will, however, mention one thing off the bat.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=65)** In version 30 of SPSS, there is a bug with a procedure that goes on behind the scenes in doing this hierarchical classification, it's the proximities command that's important for getting the multidimensional distance between spaces.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=83)** Fortunately, there is a fix, and so if you're using version 30.0.0.0, it's not going to work until you come and install this fix right here.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=97)** All you need to do is do a web search for SPSS proximities command failing in statistics 30.0 and you'll get there.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=107)** Download this and install it, and then this command will function properly.
>

>
> **[7:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=456)** This also lets us know that there's more than one way to categorize the states even on personality variables, but this approach of k-means is a good way of taking a small set of variables and using it to come up with certain commonalities in the data that you can use, again, for marketing segmentation or for medical research, or in this case, to compare it to existing categories that are in the data to see if you can get greater insights into what's happening with your cases.

> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=272)** Partitioning is a concept that may not be familiar to many people with a standard statistical background.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=278)** This is a very common approach in data science and machine learning.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=282)** The idea here is you want to make sure that your model, that your way that you're building things up, transfers well to new data.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=290)** And so, if you have a large dataset, and that's one of the reasons I switched over to this dataset, which has about 6,400 cases.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=297)** What partitioning does is cuts out some of the data and it puts it off to the side and it doesn't analyze it.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=305)** It analyzes the rest of the data, builds a model, and then it tests the model on that other dataset.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=311)** There are two common ways to do this.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=313)** One is with what you can call holdout partitioning, and that's what we're going to use right here.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=318)** 70% of the data goes into the training.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=321)** 30% goes into holdout.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=323)** If you have a variable that you've already created to partition, you can use it here.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=327)** The other major method is what's called cross validation.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=331)** And this is where you split the data, for instance, into 10 groups.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=336)** And you build a model on 9, you test it on the 10th, and then you rotate it around and you do this 10 times.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/k-nearest-neighbors-classification?u=76281980&t=342)** So, every one of the 10 groups gets to be the holdout part at one point.

> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/decision-tree-classification-in-spss?u=76281980&t=207)** And it's the testing accuracy.

> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-one-mean-to-a-population-one-sample-t-test?u=76281980&t=8)** And the way that that is usually tested is with a One-Sample T Test.

>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-one-mean-to-a-population-one-sample-t-test?u=76281980&t=176)** Cohen's d would be a common measure here, and we see that it is a small effect size, but it is reliably different from zero.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-one-mean-to-a-population-one-sample-t-test?u=76281980&t=184)** And so this is one really good way to get started in terms of looking at the mean of a single variable at once to see if it is deviating from what you would expect by random chance.

#### Comparing paired means: Paired-samples t test
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-paired-means-paired-samples-t-test?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-paired-means-paired-samples-t-test?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] People spend a lot of time trying to improve things in their lives.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-paired-means-paired-samples-t-test?u=76281980&t=3)** They want to get smarter, they want to develop a new skill, they want to lose weight, they want to get more fit.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-paired-means-paired-samples-t-test?u=76281980&t=9)** And in those situations, people are often looking at time one, before, and time two, after.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-paired-means-paired-samples-t-test?u=76281980&t=16)** If you want to look at this statistically, you would use a paired T test.
>

> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=374)** That's something that's called multicollinearity, and it complicates the interpretation, really the calculation and interpretation of multiple regressions.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=385)** So we're going to stick to a very simple version.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=387)** And in the next video, we'll look at ways of dealing with groups of variables that are correlated, and ways of selecting predictors.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=395)** But right now, we see that there are a lot of things here that are potentially correlated.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=400)** Now, let's go to linear regression and just do the most basic analysis possible.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=407)** Let's come up here to Analyze, and come down to Regression.
>
> **[6:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=411)** Then we're going to go to Linear, the second option.
>

>
> **[7:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=421)** Dependent and independent are usually reserved for randomized experiments, this definitely is not.
>
> **[7:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=426)** But let's put Job Satisfaction right there.
>

>
> **[7:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=431)** And we're going to just pick everything else and move it into Independent.
>
> **[7:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=435)** And then, this one method talks about are you going to enter them all at once, or you're going to do some sort of sequential testing.
>
> **[7:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=442)** We're doing the simplest version, which is everything all at once.
>
> **[7:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=445)** And we're not going to do any other options, I'm just going to press Okay.
>
> **[7:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=451)** So, let's take a look at the output that we have here.
>
> **[7:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=455)** First off, we have a record of the variables that were entered or removed from the equation.
>
> **[7:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=460)** And because the way we did this is just throw everything in there all at once, you see we have this long list of variables with both their names and their labels.
>
> **[7:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=469)** And then down here, it says, "Dependent Variable: Jobsat, or Job Satisfaction."
>
> **[7:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=475)** And then the next thing we have is a Model Summary, which critically gives us the multiple R, that's the multiple correlation, and in this case, it's 0.492.
>
> **[8:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=486)** Now, that's going to be useful in some situations, but what we really want to look at here is the R Square.
>
> **[8:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=493)** That is the coefficient of determination, and it tells us approximately what percentage of the variance in the outcome, job satisfaction, can be predicted by the collection of variables that we're using.
>
> **[8:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=504)** And it's about 24%; 0.242 read as a proportion is 24%.
>
> **[8:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=511)** Now, because we have a large sample size, 6,400 cases, the Adjusted R Square is going to be very similar.
>
> **[8:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=518)** And this is really the one you should be using.
>
> **[8:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=521)** The Adjusted R Square takes into consideration the number of predictor variables you have, as well as the number of cases.
>
> **[8:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=527)** Because if you have a lot of predictors and a small dataset, you're really capitalizing on the potential for some fluke chance interactions.
>
> **[8:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=537)** We have a large dataset relative to the number of predictors, so we're in pretty good shape, and they're close.
>
> **[9:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=542)** So we're predicting about 1/4 of the variance in the outcome variable, and that's good.
>
> **[9:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=547)** And if we come down to this next table, ANOVA, for Analysis of Variance, this lets us know right here that our model can predict at better than random.
>
> **[9:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=559)** It's a statistically significant model, and that's nice to hear.
>
> **[9:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=563)** The F-value for that is nearly 85, the Significance is less than 0.001.
>
> **[9:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=570)** And then we come down to a list of Coefficients.
>
> **[9:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=575)** And the one we're looking at here is first the name and label for the variable, and then we have the actual coefficient for it.
>
> **[9:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=585)** Then we have a Standardized Coefficient, which is really helpful because it allows us to compare predictors on different scales, and we have those in here.
>
> **[9:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=593)** We have a lot of 0.1 predictors here, but you can use those in regression.
>
> **[9:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=598)** And we have other ones like age, which vary tremendously.
>
> **[10:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=601)** This allows us to make a comparison between them more directly.
>
> **[10:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=605)** This column right here, t, is the actual inferential test used for each of the regression coefficients.
>
> **[10:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=613)** And then this last column, Sig, is the significance level or the probability value associated with that value of t.
>
> **[10:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=620)** And if we go down here and look for things that are less than the standard 0.05, well, we can see that the constant significant household income is significant, marital status is significant, price of primary car, and so on, lots of 'em.
>
> **[10:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=636)** But as I mentioned earlier, this is with all of these predictors that are associated with each other, which potentially complicates the interpretation of the regression.
>
> **[10:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-a-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=647)** And so in the next video, I'm going to show you several different ways of selecting through, sorting through these predictors to find something that might be more useful for your particular purposes.

> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/variable-selection?u=76281980&t=375)** And what you have here are unstandardized B or the regression coefficients for each of these.

> **[10:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/logistic-regression?u=76281980&t=607)** And the more they search for basketball, the less likely that state has a Major League Soccer team.
>
> **[10:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/logistic-regression?u=76281980&t=613)** And that's one way to take the methods of multiple regression and imply them to a novel situation where we have a yes no binary or dichotomous outcome as opposed to a scaled one.
>
> **[10:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/logistic-regression?u=76281980&t=627)** And it's another way to get actionable insight that depending on your purposes, could be very helpful in reaching your goals.

>

>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=369)** What it's done is it's computed many different models, and it does this by randomly selecting cases to include.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=375)** In each model you can see that we have 48 as our reference, but it starts going down to 31, excluding 17, and so on and so forth, and then we come down and we get a new table.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=386)** This is another interactive one.

>
> **[6:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=404)** This is an enormous improvement by getting many models and combining the results.

> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=409)** Now, there are some other things that we can look at here.

> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=420)** This one tells us how often that predictor showed up in the 10 different models that it created.

> **[7:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=429)** Here we see the ensemble accuracy and it mostly increasing over time.

> **[7:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=435)** Here's an interesting one.

>
> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=440)** Sometimes they're horrible, 3.5% or 0% accuracy, so they were awful.
>
> **[7:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=444)** But when they get combined, they end up with something that is much, much higher, nearly 81% by putting together a whole collection of weak predictors to get one very strong overall predictor.
>
> **[7:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=459)** And then we can look again, the data preparation that goes through, and it's similar to what we had before, trimming outliers and merging categories.
>
> **[7:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=465)** But all told what this does is the automatic linear modeling method, either in its default general model, or in its maximized accuracy.
>
> **[7:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=478)** It's a very sophisticated way of preparing data and creating a model that is maximized for prediction as opposed to insight.
>
> **[8:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=487)** You can save the scores to your dataset, you can export the model and apply it to new cases.
>
> **[8:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=492)** And this is a really wonderful example of high-end machine learning as implemented in SPSS.
>
> **[8:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/automatic-linear-modeling?u=76281980&t=500)** Again, depending on your goals, this may be one of the best methods to help you get the insights and the next steps you need out of your data to reach your own purposes.

>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/next-steps?u=76281980&t=47)** In addition, I have another course, Data Literacy, which covers some of the fundamental principles of working with data and using it to make decisions.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/next-steps?u=76281980&t=56)** And you can build on that with another course called [[Actionable Insights and Business Data in Practice]], which instead of teaching you how to use software or how to do particular procedures, teaches you how to make your results actionable or usable within an applied business setting.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/next-steps?u=76281980&t=75)** You can also reach out to other people through user groups, through meetup groups, and in-person events to connect with them, find out how they're using SPSS, how they're using data to make important decisions, and you could learn and potentially collaborate with them.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/spss-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/next-steps?u=76281980&t=91)** But more than anything, get out there, see what you can do, get some data on a topic that's important or interesting to you, and start exploring how you can use SPSS to get insight and make your work both exciting and more engaging.
